<p>The <b>Union for a Popular Movement</b> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr"><i>Union pour un mouvement populaire</i></span> <small>French pronunciation: ​</small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_French" title="Help:IPA for French">[y.njɔ̃.pu.ʁœ̃.muv.mɑ̃.pɔ.pyˈlɛʁ]</a></span>; <b>UMP</b> <small>French pronunciation: ​</small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_French" title="Help:IPA for French">[y.ɛmˈpe]</a></span>) was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centre-right" title="Centre-right">centre-right</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-centreright_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-centreright-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_France" title="List of political parties in France">political party in France</a> that was one of the two <a href="/wiki/Major_party" title="Major party">major</a> contemporary political parties in France along with the centre-left <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Party_(France)" title="Socialist Party (France)">Socialist Party</a> (PS). The UMP was formed in 2002 as a merger of several centre-right parties under the leadership of President <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Chirac" title="Jacques Chirac">Jacques Chirac</a>. In May 2015, the party was renamed and succeeded by <a href="/wiki/The_Republicans_(France)" title="The Republicans (France)">the Republicans</a> (<i><span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">Les Républicains</span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yahoo1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-yahoo1-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a>, then the president of the UMP, was elected <a href="/wiki/President_of_France" title="President of France">President of France</a> in the <a href="/wiki/French_presidential_election,_2007" title="French presidential election, 2007">2007 presidential election</a>, but was defeated by PS candidate <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Hollande" title="François Hollande">François Hollande</a> in a run-off <a href="/wiki/French_presidential_election,_2012" title="French presidential election, 2012">five years later</a>. After the November 2012 party congress, the UMP experienced internal fractioning and was plagued by monetary scandals which forced its president, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Cop%C3%A9" title="Jean-François Copé">Jean-François Copé</a>, to resign. After his re-election as UMP president in November 2014, Sarkozy put forward an amendment to change the name of the party into The Republicans, which was approved and came into effect on 30 May 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yahoo1_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-yahoo1-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>The UMP enjoyed an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_majority" title="Absolute majority">absolute majority</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_National_Assembly" title="French National Assembly">National Assembly</a> from 2002 to 2012 and was a member of the <a href="/wiki/European_People%27s_Party" title="European People's Party">European People's Party</a> (EPP), the <a href="/wiki/Centrist_Democrat_International" title="Centrist Democrat International">Centrist Democrat International</a> (CDI) and the <a href="/wiki/International_Democrat_Union" title="International Democrat Union">International Democrat Union</a> (IDU).</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the 1980s, the political groups of the parliamentary right have joined forces around the values of <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economic liberalism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_integration" title="European integration">building of Europe</a>. Their rivalries had contributed to their defeat in the <a href="/wiki/French_legislative_election,_1981" title="French legislative election, 1981">1981</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_legislative_election,_1988" title="French legislative election, 1988">1988</a> legislative elections.</p>
<p>Before the <a href="/wiki/French_legislative_election,_1993" title="French legislative election, 1993">1993 legislative election</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gaullism" title="Gaullism">Gaullist</a> <a href="/wiki/Rally_for_the_Republic" title="Rally for the Republic">Rally for the Republic</a> (RPR) and the centrist <a href="/wiki/Union_for_French_Democracy" title="Union for French Democracy">Union for French Democracy</a> (UDF) formed an electoral alliance, the <a href="/wiki/Union_for_France" title="Union for France">Union for France</a> (UPF). However, in the <a href="/wiki/French_presidential_election,_1995" title="French presidential election, 1995">1995 presidential campaign</a> they were both divided between followers of <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Chirac" title="Jacques Chirac">Jacques Chirac</a>, who was eventually elected, and supporters of <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_France" title="Prime Minister of France">Prime Minister</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edouard_Balladur" title="Edouard Balladur">Edouard Balladur</a>. After their defeat in the <a href="/wiki/French_legislative_election,_1997" title="French legislative election, 1997">1997 legislative election</a>, the RPR and UDF created the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Alliance_for_France&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Alliance for France (page does not exist)">Alliance for France</a> in order to coordinate the actions of their parliamentary groups.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundation_and_early_years">Foundation and early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Foundation and early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Before the <a href="/wiki/French_presidential_election,_2002" title="French presidential election, 2002">2002 presidential campaign</a>, the supporters of President <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Chirac" title="Jacques Chirac">Jacques Chirac</a>, divided in three centre-right parliamentary parties, founded an association named <b>Union on the Move</b> (<i>Union en mouvement</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ump-history_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ump-history-8">[8]</a></sup> After Chirac's re-election, in order to contest the <a href="/wiki/French_legislative_election,_2002" title="French legislative election, 2002">legislative election</a> jointly, the <b>Union for the Presidential Majority</b> (<i>Union pour la majorité présidentielle</i>) was created. It was renamed "Union for a Popular Movement" and as such established as a permanent organisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ump-history_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-ump-history-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The UMP was the merger of the Gaullist-conservative <a href="/wiki/Rally_for_the_Republic" title="Rally for the Republic">Rally for the Republic</a> (RPR), the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_liberalism" title="Conservative liberalism">conservative-liberal</a> party <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democracy_(France)" title="Liberal Democracy (France)">Liberal Democracy</a> (DL), a sizeable portion of the <a href="/wiki/Union_for_French_Democracy" title="Union for French Democracy">Union for French Democracy</a> (UDF),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EthridgeHandelman2009_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-EthridgeHandelman2009-9">[9]</a></sup> more precisely the UDF's Christian Democrats (such as <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Douste-Blazy" title="Philippe Douste-Blazy">Philippe Douste-Blazy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Barrot" title="Jacques Barrot">Jacques Barrot</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Radical_Party_(France)" title="Radical Party (France)">Radical Party</a> and the centrist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Popular_Party_for_French_Democracy" title="Popular Party for French Democracy">Popular Party for French Democracy</a> (both associate parties of the UDF until 2002). In the UMP four major French political families were thus represented: <a href="/wiki/Gaullism" title="Gaullism">Gaullism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a> (the kind of <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a> put forward by parties like the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republican_Alliance" title="Democratic Republican Alliance">Democratic Republican Alliance</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(France)" title="Republican Party (France)">PR</a>, heir of DL), <a href="/wiki/Christian_democracy" title="Christian democracy">Christian democracy</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Popolarismo" title="Popolarismo">Popularism</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Radicalism_(historical)" title="Radicalism (historical)">radicalism</a>.</p>
<p>Chirac's close ally <a href="/wiki/Alain_Jupp%C3%A9" title="Alain Juppé">Alain Juppé</a> became the party's first president at the party's founding congress at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Le_Bourget_(Seine-Saint-Denis)" title="Le Bourget (Seine-Saint-Denis)">Bourget</a> in November 2002. Juppé won 79.42% of the vote, defeating <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Dupont-Aignan" title="Nicolas Dupont-Aignan">Nicolas Dupont-Aignan</a>, the leader of the party's Eurosceptic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arise_the_Republic" title="Arise the Republic">Arise the Republic</a> faction, and three other candidates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ump-history_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-ump-history-8">[8]</a></sup> During the party's earlier years, it was marked by tensions and rivalries between Juppé and other <i>chiraquiens</i> and supporters of <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a>, the then-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Interior_(France)" title="Ministry of Interior (France)">Minister of the Interior</a>.</p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/French_regional_elections,_2004" title="French regional elections, 2004">2004 regional elections</a> the UMP suffered a heavy blow, winning the presidencies of only 2 out of 22 regions in metropolitan France (<a href="/wiki/Alsace" title="Alsace">Alsace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a>) and only half of the departments (the right had previously won numerous departmental presidencies) in the simultaneous <a href="/wiki/French_cantonal_elections,_2004" title="French cantonal elections, 2004">2004 cantonal elections</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2004_(France)" title="European Parliament election, 2004 (France)">2004 European Parliament election</a> on 13 June 2004, the UMP also suffered another heavy blow, winning 16.6% of the vote, far behind the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Party_(France)" title="Socialist Party (France)">Socialist Party</a> (PS), and only 16 seats.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nicolas_Sarkozy_.282004.E2.80.932012.29">Nicolas Sarkozy (2004–2012)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Nicolas Sarkozy (2004–2012)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Juppé resigned the party's presidency on 15 July 2004 after being found guilty in a <a href="/wiki/Political_corruption" title="Political corruption">corruption scandal</a> in January of the same year. Nicolas Sarkozy rapidly announced that he would take over the presidency of the UMP and resign his position as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minister_of_the_Economy,_Finance_and_Industry_(France)" title="Minister of the Economy, Finance and Industry (France)">finance minister</a>, ending months of speculation. On 28 November 2004, Sarkozy was elected to the party's presidency with 85.09% of the votes against 9.1% for Dupont-Aignan and 5.82% for <a href="/wiki/Christine_Boutin" title="Christine Boutin">Christine Boutin</a>, the leader of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forum_of_Social_Republicans" title="Forum of Social Republicans">UMP's social conservatives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ump-history_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-ump-history-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Having gained control of what had been Chirac's party, Sarkozy focused the party machinery and his energies on the <a href="/wiki/French_presidential_election,_2007" title="French presidential election, 2007">2007 presidential election</a>.</p>
<p>The failure of the <a href="/wiki/French_European_Constitution_referendum,_2005" title="French European Constitution referendum, 2005">referendum on the European Constitution</a> on 25 May 2005 led to the fall of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Raffarin_Government&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Raffarin Government (page does not exist)">government of Jean-Pierre Raffarin</a> and to the formation of a new cabinet, presided by another UMP politician, <a href="/wiki/Dominique_de_Villepin" title="Dominique de Villepin">Dominique de Villepin</a>. However, during this time, the UMP under Sarkozy gained a record number of new members and rejuvenated itself in preparation of the 2007 election. On 14 January 2007, Sarkozy was nominated unopposed as the UMP's presidential candidate for the 2007 election.</p>
<p>On the issues, the party under Sarkozy publicly disapproved of <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>'s proposed membership in the European Union, which Chirac had previously endorsed several times publicly, and generally took a more <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_politics" title="Right-wing politics">right-wing</a> position.</p>
<p>On 22 April 2007 <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a> won the plurality of votes in the first round of the <a href="/wiki/French_presidential_election,_2007" title="French presidential election, 2007">2007 presidential election</a>. On 6 May he faced the Socialist Party candidate <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A9gol%C3%A8ne_Royal" title="Ségolène Royal">Ségolène Royal</a> in the second round and won, taking 53.06% of the vote. As a consequence, he resigned from the presidency of the UMP on 14 May 2007, two days before becoming President of the French Republic. <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Fillon" title="François Fillon">François Fillon</a> was appointed Prime Minister. On 17 June 2007, at a <a href="/wiki/French_legislative_election,_2007" title="French legislative election, 2007">2007 legislative election</a>, the UMP gained a majority in the National Assembly with 313 out of 577 seats.</p>
<p>Following Sarkozy's election to the presidency, interim leader <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Gaudin" title="Jean-Claude Gaudin">Jean-Claude Gaudin</a> prevented a leadership struggle between <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Devedjian" title="Patrick Devedjian">Patrick Devedjian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Raffarin" title="Jean-Pierre Raffarin">Jean-Pierre Raffarin</a> by announcing that the UMP should have a collegial leadership while Sarkozy was President of the Republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> In July, the UMP's national council approved an amendment to the party's statute allowing for a collegial leadership around three vice-presidents (Jean-Pierre Raffarin, Jean-Claude Gaudin and <a href="/wiki/Pierre_M%C3%A9haignerie" title="Pierre Méhaignerie">Pierre Méhaignerie</a>) and a secretary-general (<a href="/wiki/Patrick_Devedjian" title="Patrick Devedjian">Patrick Devedjian</a>) and two associate secretary-generals.</p>
<p>In the 9 March 2008 <a href="/wiki/French_municipal_elections,_2008" title="French municipal elections, 2008">municipal</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_cantonal_elections,_2008" title="French cantonal elections, 2008">cantonal</a> elections the party performed quite poorly, losing numerous cities, such as <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">Toulouse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a>, as well as eight departmental presidencies to the left. <a href="/wiki/Xavier_Bertrand" title="Xavier Bertrand">Xavier Bertrand</a> was selected as secretary-general of the party in late 2008 to replace <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Devedjian" title="Patrick Devedjian">Patrick Devedjian</a>, who resigned to take up a cabinet position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2009_(France)" title="European Parliament election, 2009 (France)">2009 European Parliament election</a> on 7 June 2009, the UMP ran common lists with its junior allies including <a href="/wiki/Jean-Louis_Borloo" title="Jean-Louis Borloo">Jean-Louis Borloo</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Radical_Party_(France)" title="Radical Party (France)">Radical Party</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_Centre" title="New Centre">New Centre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_Left" title="Modern Left">Modern Left</a>. The UMP list won 27.9%, a remarkably good result for a governing party in off-year "mid-term" elections, and elected 29 MEPs, significantly improving on the UMP's poor result in the 2004 European election – also an off-year election. However, in the <a href="/wiki/French_regional_elections,_2010" title="French regional elections, 2010">2010 regional elections</a> on 14 and 21 March 2010, the UMP obtained a very poor result with only 26%. While it lost Corsica, it retained Alsace but also defeated the left in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_R%C3%A9union" title="La Réunion">La Réunion</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/French_Guiana" title="French Guiana">French Guiana</a>.</p>
<p>In a cabinet reshuffle in November 2010, which disappointed centrists within and outside the UMP, François Fillon was confirmed Prime Minister and <a href="/wiki/Alain_Jupp%C3%A9" title="Alain Juppé">Alain Juppé</a> re-joined the government. Among those who resigned from the cabinet were <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Kouchner" title="Bernard Kouchner">Bernard Kouchner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herv%C3%A9_Morin" title="Hervé Morin">Hervé Morin</a> and, above all, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Louis_Borloo" title="Jean-Louis Borloo">Jean-Louis Borloo</a>. Xavier Bertrand, who re-joined the government, was replaced as general-secretary of the UMP by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Cop%C3%A9" title="Jean-François Copé">Jean-François Copé</a> on 17 November 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The party suffered another major electoral defeat in the <a href="/wiki/French_cantonal_elections,_2011" title="French cantonal elections, 2011">2011 cantonal elections</a> held on 20 and 27 March 2011, and in September, the centre-right lost control of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Senate" title="French Senate">French Senate</a> for the first time in the history of the Fifth Republic.</p>
<p>In May 2011, during a party congress, the Radical Party, led by Borloo, decided to leave the UMP and launch <a href="/wiki/The_Alliance_(France)" title="The Alliance (France)">The Alliance</a>, a new <a href="/wiki/Centrism" title="Centrism">centrist</a> coalition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>The party opted not to organise primaries ahead of the <a href="/wiki/French_presidential_election,_2012" title="French presidential election, 2012">2012 presidential election</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> and endorsed <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a>'s bid for second term. Sarkozy lost reelection to the Socialist Party candidate <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Hollande" title="François Hollande">François Hollande</a> on 6 May 2012, winning 48.36% in the runoff. The party was defeated by the new President's left-wing majority in the subsequent <a href="/wiki/French_legislative_election,_2012" title="French legislative election, 2012">legislative election</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_May_2012">After May 2012</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: After May 2012">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Prior to Sarkozy's defeat on 6 May, the UMP's secretary-general Jean-François Copé announced that he supported the creation of internal "movements" within the party<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and the organisation of primaries for the next presidential election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_for_the_November_2012_congress">Campaign for the November 2012 congress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Campaign for the November 2012 congress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The UMP's political bureau announced the organisation of a party congress on 18 and 25 November 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> leading prominent party leaders to organise factions and "movements" to influence the party's new direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ultimately, two candidates amassed the required endorsements to run for the party's presidency: former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Fillon" title="François Fillon">François Fillon</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> and incumbent party secretary-general <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Cop%C3%A9" title="Jean-François Copé">Jean-François Copé</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nathalie_Kosciusko-Morizet" title="Nathalie Kosciusko-Morizet">Nathalie Kosciusko-Morizet</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Le_Maire" title="Bruno Le Maire">Bruno Le Maire</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Xavier_Bertrand" title="Xavier Bertrand">Xavier Bertrand</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henri_Guaino" title="Henri Guaino">Henri Guaino</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Dominique_Dord" title="Dominique Dord">Dominique Dord</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> had also announced their candidacies but did not meet tough candidacy requirements.</p>
<p>The campaign between Fillon and Copé lasted two months. Fillon had a strong lead in polls of UMP 'sympathizers' (as opposed to actual members, who would be the only eligible voters) and was backed by most UMP parliamentarians<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> while Copé claimed he was the candidate of party activists rather than party 'barons'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> However, Copé remained as secretary-general and retained control of the party machinery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>While Fillon's campaign was regarded as more consensual, moderate and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centre-right" title="Centre-right">centre-right</a>; Copé campaigned as the candidate of the <i>droite décomplexée</i> ('uninhibited right')<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> and introduced issues such as <a href="/wiki/Reverse_racism" title="Reverse racism">anti-white racism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> However, both candidates received support from moderate and conservative members of the party and their main differences were in rhetoric, style and temperament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Copé, again, appeared more militant and activist, saying that he would support and participate in street demonstrations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> while Fillon disagreed with his rival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Six 'motions' (declarations of principles) were submitted to party voters; under the new statutes, motions which won over 10% of the vote at the congress would be recognised as "movements" by the UMP leadership, granted financial autonomy and receive positions in the party structures.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Results_and_subsequent_crisis">Results and subsequent crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Results and subsequent crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The vote on 18 November saw high turnout but was quickly marred by allegations of irregularities and potential fraud on both sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Both candidates proclaimed victory within 20 minutes of each other on the night of the vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>24 hours later, the control commission in charge of the vote (COCOE) announced Copé's victory by only 98 votes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> While Fillon initially conceded defeat, by 21 November his campaign claimed victory anew, with a 26-vote advantage over Copé.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Fillon's campaign argued that the COCOE had failed to take into account votes cast in three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DOM-TOM" title="DOM-TOM">overseas federations</a>.</p>
<p>Party elder Alain Juppé accepted to lead a mediation between both candidates on 23 November,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> but it failed within two days. Fillon's announced "precautionary seizure" of ballots cast "to protect them from tampering or alteration"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-businessinsider.com_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-businessinsider.com-44">[44]</a></sup> and threatened to take the matter to court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> On 26 November, the party appeals commission - led by a close supporter of Copé - decided in Copé's favour and rejected Fillon's arguments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 27 November, 72 <i>filloniste</i> parliamentarians in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_National_Assembly" title="French National Assembly">National Assembly</a> announced the creation of a new parliamentary group, the <i>Rassemblement-UMP</i>, led by Fillon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Copé took up former President Nicolas Sarkozy's proposal of organising a <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">referendum</a> on a revote, but he saw the creation of the dissident <i>filloniste</i> group as a <a href="/wiki/Casus_belli" title="Casus belli">casus belli</a> and took back his proposal. <a href="/wiki/Luc_Chatel" title="Luc Chatel">Luc Chatel</a>, the new vice-president and a Copé supporter, later announced that he supported a new presidential vote and a modification of party statutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The next day, Copé announced that he favoured organising a referendum the modification of party statutes and a reduction of his own term as president to two years (until November 2014); while Fillon welcomed the "consensus on the organisation of a new election" he rejected his rival's timeline and called for a new election before 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> 'Unaligned' members of the UMP led by <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Le_Maire" title="Bruno Le Maire">Bruno Le Maire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nathalie_Kosciusko-Morizet" title="Nathalie Kosciusko-Morizet">Nathalie Kosciusko-Morizet</a> appealed for the organisation of a new election in the spring of 2013 and a reform of the party statutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Resolution">Resolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Resolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Both rivals reached an agreement at the end of December 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> with Copé agreeing to the organisation of a new election and a modification of party statutes while Fillon agreed to dissolve his parliamentary group.</p>
<p>The party's leadership was reorganized in January 2013 to accommodate Copé and Fillon's supporters: <a href="/wiki/Laurent_Wauquiez" title="Laurent Wauquiez">Laurent Wauquiez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Val%C3%A9rie_P%C3%A9cresse" title="Valérie Pécresse">Valérie Pécresse</a> joined <a href="/wiki/Luc_Chatel" title="Luc Chatel">Luc Chatel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mich%C3%A8le_Tabarot" title="Michèle Tabarot">Michèle Tabarot</a> as vice-president and secretary-general respectively. <a href="/wiki/Christian_Estrosi" title="Christian Estrosi">Christian Estrosi</a>, <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9rard_Longuet" title="Gérard Longuet">Gérard Longuet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henri_de_Raincourt" title="Henri de Raincourt">Henri de Raincourt</a> (pro-Fillon), <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Gaudin" title="Jean-Claude Gaudin">Jean-Claude Gaudin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brice_Hortefeux" title="Brice Hortefeux">Brice Hortefeux</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roger_Karoutchi" title="Roger Karoutchi">Roger Karoutchi</a> (pro-Copé) also became vice-presidents. Other positions in the party hierarchy were divided between supporters of both candidates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> New leaders were also nominated in February 2013.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bygmalion_Scandal">Bygmalion Scandal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Bygmalion Scandal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Several spending scandals appeared in 2014. In early 2014, the Bygmalion scandal (<a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bygmalion#Affaire_UMP-Bygmalion" title="fr:Bygmalion">fr</a>) pushed the party's leader <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Cop%C3%A9" title="Jean-François Copé">Jean-François Copé</a> to resign. In early July, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarkozy" title="Sarkozy">Sarkozy</a> got held in custody due to possible spying and active corruption of the judiciary system. On 8 July 2014, the UMP was discovered to have a hidden debt of €79.1 million for the year 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_change_and_dissolution">Name change and dissolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Name change and dissolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the election of <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a>, the former <a href="/wiki/President_of_France" title="President of France">President of France</a> (2007–2012), as president of the Union for a Popular Movement (UMP) in November 2014, he put forward a request to the party's general committee to change its name to the Republicans as well as the statutes of the party. With the name already chosen <a href="/wiki/Nathalie_Kosciusko-Morizet" title="Nathalie Kosciusko-Morizet">Nathalie Kosciusko-Morizet</a>, vice president of the UMP, presented <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a> and the party's political bureau a project of new statutes. The proposed statutes provided for, among others, the election of the presidents of the departmental federations by direct suffrage, the end of the political currents and consulting members on election nominations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics of Sarkozy claimed it was illegal for him to name the party "Republicans" because every French person is a republican in that they support the values and ideals of the <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French Republic</a> that emanated from the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, and as such the term is above party politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The new name was adopted by the bureau on 5 May 2015 and approved by the party membership on 28 May by an online <b>yes</b> vote of 83,28 % on a 45,74 % participation after a court ruling in favor of Sarkozy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LP3052015_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-LP3052015-56">[56]</a></sup> Similarly the new party statutes are adopted by 96.34% of voters and the composition of the new party's political bureau by 94.77%. <a href="/wiki/The_Republicans_(France)" title="The Republicans (France)">The Republicans</a> thus became the legal successor of the UMP as the leading centre-right party in France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yahoo1_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-yahoo1-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideology">Ideology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ideology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The UMP was a <a href="/wiki/Centre-right_politics" title="Centre-right politics">centre-right</a> party and was created to represent the various families of the French right, uniting the traditions of <a href="/wiki/Gaullism" title="Gaullism">Gaullism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_democracy" title="Christian democracy">Christian democracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">conservatism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a>. As such, it officially adopted fairly vague policy statements which emphasize consensual common values.</p>
<p>The UMP believed that each individual's <a href="/wiki/Destiny" title="Destiny">destiny</a> must be unencumbered and it rejects political systems which "stifle economic freedom". It said that work, merit, innovation and personal initiative must be encouraged to reduce <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment</a> and boost <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">economic growth</a>; but at the same time, it maintained that adherence to the rule of law and the authority of the state is necessary. In a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaullist" title="Gaullist">Gaullist</a> tradition, the UMP supported solidarity, with the state guaranteeing social protection of less fortunate individuals. But in a more <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a> vein, the party always denounced <i>l'assistanat</i>, a French term which can refer to "<a href="/wiki/Welfare" title="Welfare">welfare</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Handouts" title="Handouts">handouts</a>".</p>
<p>The party took more nationalist positions at times, and often adopted tough stances against <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Illegal_immigration" title="Illegal immigration">illegal immigration</a>. It strongly supported the integration and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">assimilation</a> of immigrants into French society and always denounced <a href="/wiki/Communitarianism" title="Communitarianism">communitarianism</a> as a danger to the French <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nation-state" title="Nation-state">nation-state</a>. However, the UMP traditionally was a strong proponent of <a href="/wiki/European_integration" title="European integration">European integration</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, albeit sometimes with a hint of traditional Gaullist <a href="/wiki/Souverainism" title="Souverainism">souverainism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a>'s leadership, the UMP adopted a liberal and security-oriented platform. His platform in the <a href="/wiki/French_presidential_election,_2007" title="French presidential election, 2007">2007</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_presidential_election,_2012" title="French presidential election, 2012">2012</a> presidential elections emphasized the ideas of personal responsibility and individual initiative. He developed the idea of "working more to earn more", promising that <a href="/wiki/Overtime" title="Overtime">overtime</a> hours would not be taxed and employers exonerated from <a href="/wiki/Non-wage_labour_costs" title="Non-wage labour costs">non-wage labour costs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrechonPierre_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrechonPierre-58">[58]</a></sup> Under his presidency, the government's short-lived <a href="/wiki/Tax_cap" title="Tax cap">tax cap</a> for high-income earners was denounced by the left but also several centrist and centre-right politicians within or outside the UMP.</p>
<p>Having gained his popularity as a 'hardliner' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ministry_of_the_Interior_(France)" title="Ministry of the Interior (France)">Interior minister</a>, Sarkozy's policies also carried a strong law-and-order and tough on crime orientation. He supported tougher sentences for criminals and <a href="/wiki/Recidivism" title="Recidivism">repeat offenders</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrechonPierre_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-BrechonPierre-58">[58]</a></sup> As candidate and President, he placed heavy emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_identity" title="National identity">national identity</a>, presenting immigration as a danger to French identity and as source of increased criminality. As President, he imposed stricter limits on <a href="/wiki/Family_reunification" title="Family reunification">family reunification</a>, created a <a href="/wiki/French_Ministry_of_Immigration,_Integration,_National_Identity_and_Codevelopment" title="French Ministry of Immigration, Integration, National Identity and Codevelopment">Ministry of Immigration, and National Identity</a> for three years between 2007 and 2010, launched a controversial national dialogue on national identity and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Roma_expulsion" title="French Roma expulsion">expelled thousands</a> of <a href="/wiki/Romani_people" title="Romani people">Roma</a> from illegal camps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrechonPierre_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-BrechonPierre-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics of the right-wing government denounced what they felt was a rapprochement with the controversial far-right <a href="/wiki/National_Front_(France)" title="National Front (France)">National Front</a> (FN). While several members of the UMP's right-wing have indicated that they would favour local alliances with the FN and prefer to vote for a FN candidate over a <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Party_(France)" title="Socialist Party (France)">Socialist Party</a> or left-wing candidate in runoff elections between the left and the FN; the party's official position continues to reject alliances with the FN at any level but also opposes so-called "republican fronts" with the left against the FN.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Factions">Factions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Factions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The UMP's original statutes in 2002 allowed for the organisation of formal factions or movements within the party, to represent the various political families of which it was made up. However, fearing leadership rivalries and divisions, Juppé, Chirac and later Sarkozy 'postponed' the creation of such organised movements indefinitely. Nevertheless, prior to the organisations of formal "movements" in November 2012, there existed informal groupings of like-minded members, either through associations, political clubs, associated political parties or even informal factions.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Cop%C3%A9" title="Jean-François Copé">Jean-François Copé</a> allowed for the organisation of formal movements within the party following the November 2012 congress. According to the party's statutes, motions backed by at least 10 parliamentarians from 10 departmental federations and which obtain at least 10% support from members at a congress are recognised as movements. They are granted financial autonomy by way of a fixed grant and additional funding in proportion to the votes they obtained; but the sum of funds transferred by the party to its movements can be no larger than 30% of the annual public subsidies the UMP receives from the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_movement_and_factions">Official movement and factions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Official movement and factions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Six motions representing various ideological tendencies within the party ran to be recognised as official movements following the <a href="/wiki/Union_for_a_Popular_Movement_leadership_election,_2012" title="Union for a Popular Movement leadership election, 2012">November 2012 congress</a>. Five of these motions met the conditions to be recognised as such, and their leaders have since integrated the UMP's leadership structure:</p>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/The_Strong_Right" title="The Strong Right">The Strong Right</a></b> (<i>La Droite forte</i>): 27.77%
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Sarkozysm" title="Sarkozysm">Sarkozysts</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">conservatives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberal_conservatism" title="Liberal conservatism">liberal-conservatives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conservative_liberalism" title="Conservative liberalism">conservative-liberals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism" title="Social conservatism">social conservatives</a>): <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Gaudin" title="Jean-Claude Gaudin">Jean-Claude Gaudin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Raffarin" title="Jean-Pierre Raffarin">Jean-Pierre Raffarin</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Balladur" title="Édouard Balladur">Édouard Balladur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dominique_Bussereau" title="Dominique Bussereau">Dominique Bussereau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michel_Barnier" title="Michel Barnier">Michel Barnier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dominique_Perben" title="Dominique Perben">Dominique Perben</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Mattei" title="Jean-François Mattei">Jean-François Mattei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renaud_Donnedieu_de_Vabres" title="Renaud Donnedieu de Vabres">Renaud Donnedieu de Vabres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Millon" title="Charles Millon">Charles Millon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alain_Lamassoure" title="Alain Lamassoure">Alain Lamassoure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brice_Hortefeux" title="Brice Hortefeux">Brice Hortefeux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Daul" title="Joseph Daul">Joseph Daul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rachida_Dati" title="Rachida Dati">Rachida Dati</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Accoyer" title="Bernard Accoyer">Bernard Accoyer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marie-H%C3%A9l%C3%A8ne_Descamps" title="Marie-Hélène Descamps">Marie-Hélène Descamps</a></li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_Ecology" title="Blue Ecology">Blue Ecology</a></b> (centrist <a href="/wiki/Green_politics" title="Green politics">ecologists</a>): <a href="/wiki/Nathalie_Kosciusko-Morizet" title="Nathalie Kosciusko-Morizet">Nathalie Kosciusko-Morizet</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/The_Social_Right" title="The Social Right">The Social Right</a></b> (<i>La Droite sociale</i>): 21.69%
<ul>
<li><b>Social Gaullists</b> or <b>Séguinists</b> (left-wing Gaullists, <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social democrats</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurosceptics" title="Eurosceptics">Eurosceptics</a>): <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Fillon" title="François Fillon">François Fillon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Karoutchi" title="Roger Karoutchi">Roger Karoutchi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henri_Guaino" title="Henri Guaino">Henri Guaino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yves_Gu%C3%A9na" title="Yves Guéna">Yves Guéna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alain_Marleix" title="Alain Marleix">Alain Marleix</a></li>

</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Modern_and_Humanist_France" title="Modern and Humanist France">Modern and Humanist France</a></b> (<i>France moderne et humaniste</i>): 18.17%
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/The_Reformers" title="The Reformers">The Reformers</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">classical liberals</a>): <a href="/wiki/Herv%C3%A9_Novelli" title="Hervé Novelli">Hervé Novelli</a>, <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9rard_Longuet" title="Gérard Longuet">Gérard Longuet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alain_Madelin" title="Alain Madelin">Alain Madelin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Devedjian" title="Patrick Devedjian">Patrick Devedjian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Cochet" title="Philippe Cochet">Philippe Cochet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Soisson" title="Jean-Pierre Soisson">Jean-Pierre Soisson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claude_Goasguen" title="Claude Goasguen">Claude Goasguen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Lellouche" title="Pierre Lellouche">Pierre Lellouche</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luc_Chatel" title="Luc Chatel">Luc Chatel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Giscard_d%27Estaing" title="Louis Giscard d'Estaing">Louis Giscard d'Estaing</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jean-Jacques_Descamps&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jean-Jacques Descamps (page does not exist)">Jean-Jacques Descamps</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Democratic_and_Popular" title="Democratic and Popular">Democratic and Popular</a></b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Democracy" title="Christian Democracy">Christian democrats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Centrism" title="Centrism">centrists</a>): <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Douste-Blazy" title="Philippe Douste-Blazy">Philippe Douste-Blazy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_M%C3%A9haignerie" title="Pierre Méhaignerie">Pierre Méhaignerie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adrien_Zeller" title="Adrien Zeller">Adrien Zeller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Barrot" title="Jacques Barrot">Jacques Barrot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicole_Fontaine" title="Nicole Fontaine">Nicole Fontaine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marc-Philippe_Daubresse" title="Marc-Philippe Daubresse">Marc-Philippe Daubresse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alain_Joyandet" title="Alain Joyandet">Alain Joyandet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Herth" title="Antoine Herth">Antoine Herth</a></li>

<li><b><a href="/wiki/Gaullism,_a_way_forward_for_France" title="Gaullism, a way forward for France">Gaullism, a way forward for France</a></b> (<i>Le Gaullisme, une voie d'avenir pour la France</i>): 12.31%
<ul>
<li><b>Neo-Gaullists</b>, formerly known also as <b>Chiraquiens</b> (right-wing slightly liberal <a href="/wiki/Gaullism" title="Gaullism">Gaullists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secular</a>-minded conservatives): <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Chirac" title="Jacques Chirac">Jacques Chirac</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mich%C3%A8le_Alliot-Marie" title="Michèle Alliot-Marie">Michèle Alliot-Marie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Louis_Debr%C3%A9" title="Jean-Louis Debré">Jean-Louis Debré</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Cop%C3%A9" title="Jean-François Copé">Jean-François Copé</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alain_Jupp%C3%A9" title="Alain Juppé">Alain Juppé</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Ollier" title="Patrick Ollier">Patrick Ollier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Baroin" title="François Baroin">François Baroin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xavier_Bertrand" title="Xavier Bertrand">Xavier Bertrand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xavier_Darcos" title="Xavier Darcos">Xavier Darcos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Val%C3%A9rie_P%C3%A9cresse" title="Valérie Pécresse">Valérie Pécresse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christine_Albanel" title="Christine Albanel">Christine Albanel</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%89ric_W%C5%93rth" title="Éric Wœrth">Éric Wœrth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Karoutchi" title="Roger Karoutchi">Roger Karoutchi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Josselin_de_Rohan" title="Josselin de Rohan">Josselin de Rohan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adrien_Gouteyron" title="Adrien Gouteyron">Adrien Gouteyron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herv%C3%A9_Mariton" title="Hervé Mariton">Hervé Mariton</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/The_Free_Right" title="The Free Right">The Free Right</a></b> (conservative-liberals, <a href="/wiki/Souverainism" title="Souverainism">souverainists</a>): <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rachid_Kaci&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rachid Kaci (page does not exist)">Rachid Kaci</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_del_Valle" title="Alexandre del Valle">Alexandre del Valle</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Blanc" title="Étienne Blanc">Étienne Blanc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_d%27Aubert" title="François d'Aubert">François d'Aubert</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/The_Popular_Right" title="The Popular Right">The Popular Right</a></b> (<i>La Droite populaire</i>): 10.87%
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Initiative_and_Liberty_Movement" title="Initiative and Liberty Movement">Initiative and Liberty Movement</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Gaullism" title="Gaullism">Gaullists</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_conservatism" title="National conservatism">national-conservatives</a>): <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Debr%C3%A9" title="Bernard Debré">Bernard Debré</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eric_Raoult" title="Eric Raoult">Eric Raoult</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Tiberi" title="Jean Tiberi">Jean Tiberi</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Rally_for_France" title="Rally for France">Rally for France</a></b> (national-conservatives, souverainists): <a href="/wiki/Charles_Pasqua" title="Charles Pasqua">Charles Pasqua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lionnel_Luca" title="Lionnel Luca">Lionnel Luca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Myard" title="Jacques Myard">Jacques Myard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Guillet" title="Jean-Jacques Guillet">Jean-Jacques Guillet</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Philippe_Pemezec&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Philippe Pemezec (page does not exist)">Philippe Pemezec</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Associate_parties">Associate parties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Associate parties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hunting,_Fishing,_Nature,_Tradition" title="Hunting, Fishing, Nature, Tradition">Hunting, Fishing, Nature, Tradition</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Democratic_Party_(France)" title="Christian Democratic Party (France)">Christian Democratic Party</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Rally_for_France" title="Rally for France">Rally for France</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Progressives" title="The Progressives">The Progressives</a> are associate parties of the UMP. By adhering to these parties, members also adhered to the UMP and could participate in the UMP's inner organisation. The <a href="/wiki/Radical_Party_(France)" title="Radical Party (France)">Radical Party</a> was associated with the UMP from 2002 through 2011.</p>
<p>Overseas parties associated with the UMP included <a href="/wiki/O_Porinetia_To_Tatou_Ai%27a" title="O Porinetia To Tatou Ai'a">O Porinetia To Tatou Ai'a</a> in <a href="/wiki/French_Polynesia" title="French Polynesia">French Polynesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Rally%E2%80%93UMP" title="The Rally–UMP">The Rally–UMP</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2012_leadership_election">2012 leadership election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 2012 leadership election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The aforementioned <a href="/wiki/Union_for_a_Popular_Movement_leadership_election,_2012" title="Union for a Popular Movement leadership election, 2012">November 2012 congress</a> saw the division of the party between the two candidates who sought the party's presidency, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Fillon" title="François Fillon">François Fillon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Cop%C3%A9" title="Jean-François Copé">Jean-François Copé</a> - the <i>fillonistes</i> and <i>copéistes</i>.</p>
<ul>
<li><i>Copéistes</i> (supporters of <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Cop%C3%A9" title="Jean-François Copé">Jean-François Copé</a>): <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Cop%C3%A9" title="Jean-François Copé">Jean-François Copé</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luc_Chatel" title="Luc Chatel">Luc Chatel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mich%C3%A8le_Tabarot" title="Michèle Tabarot">Michèle Tabarot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Gaudin" title="Jean-Claude Gaudin">Jean-Claude Gaudin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Raffarin" title="Jean-Pierre Raffarin">Jean-Pierre Raffarin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marc-Philippe_Daubresse" title="Marc-Philippe Daubresse">Marc-Philippe Daubresse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herv%C3%A9_Novelli" title="Hervé Novelli">Hervé Novelli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Jacob_(politician)" title="Christian Jacob (politician)">Christian Jacob</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lionnel_Luca" title="Lionnel Luca">Lionnel Luca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thierry_Mariani" title="Thierry Mariani">Thierry Mariani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Peltier" title="Guillaume Peltier">Guillaume Peltier</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rachida_Dati" title="Rachida Dati">Rachida Dati</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brice_Hortefeux" title="Brice Hortefeux">Brice Hortefeux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nadine_Morano" title="Nadine Morano">Nadine Morano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Sarkozy" title="Jean Sarkozy">Jean Sarkozy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Val%C3%A9rie_Rosso-Debord" title="Valérie Rosso-Debord">Valérie Rosso-Debord</a> etc.</li>
<li><i>Fillonistes</i> (supporters of <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Fillon" title="François Fillon">François Fillon</a>): <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Fillon" title="François Fillon">François Fillon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laurent_Wauquiez" title="Laurent Wauquiez">Laurent Wauquiez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Val%C3%A9rie_P%C3%A9cresse" title="Valérie Pécresse">Valérie Pécresse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xavier_Bertrand" title="Xavier Bertrand">Xavier Bertrand</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christian_Estrosi" title="Christian Estrosi">Christian Estrosi</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89ric_Ciotti" title="Éric Ciotti">Éric Ciotti</a>, <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9rard_Larcher" title="Gérard Larcher">Gérard Larcher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Baroin" title="François Baroin">François Baroin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Devedjian" title="Patrick Devedjian">Patrick Devedjian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dominique_Bussereau" title="Dominique Bussereau">Dominique Bussereau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Val%C3%A9rie_Boyer" title="Valérie Boyer">Valérie Boyer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dominique_Dord" title="Dominique Dord">Dominique Dord</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Ollier" title="Patrick Ollier">Patrick Ollier</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89ric_Woerth" title="Éric Woerth">Éric Woerth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Falco" title="Hubert Falco">Hubert Falco</a>, <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9rard_Longuet" title="Gérard Longuet">Gérard Longuet</a> etc.</li>
<li>Unaligned members: <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Le_Maire" title="Bruno Le Maire">Bruno Le Maire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nathalie_Kosciusko-Morizet" title="Nathalie Kosciusko-Morizet">Nathalie Kosciusko-Morizet</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alain_Jupp%C3%A9" title="Alain Juppé">Alain Juppé</a> also remained neutral and did not officially endorse any candidate.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Elected_officials">Elected officials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Elected officials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><b>Deputies:</b> 186 members and 9 caucusing members in the UMP group in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_National_Assembly" title="French National Assembly">National Assembly</a>. This group also includes some members of the <a href="/wiki/Radical_Party_(France)" title="Radical Party (France)">Radical Party</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Democratic_Party_(France)" title="Christian Democratic Party (France)">Christian Democratic Party</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miscellaneous_Right" title="Miscellaneous Right">Miscellaneous Right</a> deputies.</li>
<li><b>Senators:</b> 131 members in the UMP group in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Senate" title="French Senate">Senate</a>. This group also includes members of the <a href="/wiki/Radical_Party_(France)" title="Radical Party (France)">Radical Party</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Rally-UMP" title="The Rally-UMP">The Rally-UMP</a></li>
<li><b>MEPs:</b> 24 members in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_People%27s_Party_(European_Parliament_group)" title="European People's Party (European Parliament group)">EPP Group</a> in the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_officeholders">Major officeholders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Major officeholders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President_of_the_French_Republic" title="President of the French Republic">President of the Republic</a>, 2007–2012)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Fillon" title="François Fillon">François Fillon</a> (<a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_France" title="Prime Minister of France">Prime Minister</a>, 2007–2012)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bernard_Accoyer" title="Bernard Accoyer">Bernard Accoyer</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_French_National_Assembly" title="List of Presidents of the French National Assembly">President of the National Assembly</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jean-Louis_Debr%C3%A9" title="Jean-Louis Debré">Jean-Louis Debré</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President_of_the_Constitutional_Council_of_France" title="President of the Constitutional Council of France">President of the Constitutional Council</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Daul" title="Joseph Daul">Joseph Daul</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_People%27s_Party_(European_Parliament_group)" title="European People's Party (European Parliament group)">President of the EPP Group</a> in the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a>)</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_support">Popular support</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Popular support">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The UMP's electoral base reflects that of the old <a href="/wiki/Rally_for_the_Republic" title="Rally for the Republic">Rally for the Republic</a> (RPR) and, in some cases, that of the <a href="/wiki/Union_for_French_Democracy" title="Union for French Democracy">Union for French Democracy</a> (UDF). In the 2007 presidential election, <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a> performed best in the east of France – particularly <a href="/wiki/Alsace" title="Alsace">Alsace</a> (36.2%); <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Provence-Alpes-C%C3%B4te-d%27Azur" title="Provence-Alpes-Côte-d'Azur">Provence-Alpes-Côte-d'Azur</a> (37.0%) – the wealthy coastal <a href="/wiki/Departments_of_France" title="Departments of France">department</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Alpes-Maritimes" title="Alpes-Maritimes">Alpes-Maritimes</a> (43.6%) was his best department in France; <a href="/wiki/Champagne-Ardenne" title="Champagne-Ardenne">Champagne-Ardenne</a> (32.7%) and <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne-Alpes" title="Rhône-Alpes">Rhône-Alpes</a> (32.7%). These areas were among <a href="/wiki/National_Front_(France)" title="National Front (France)">National Front</a> candidate <a href="/wiki/Jean-Marie_Le_Pen" title="Jean-Marie Le Pen">Jean-Marie Le Pen</a>'s best regions in 2002 and are conservative on issues such as immigration. Sarkozy obviously received a lot of votes from voters who had supported the far-right in April 2002. For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Alpes-Maritimes" title="Alpes-Maritimes">Alpes-Maritimes</a>, Sarkozy performed 21.6% better than Chirac did in 2002 while Le Pen lost 12.6% in five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ifop_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ifop-65">[65]</a></sup> Sarkozy also appealed more than average to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue-collar" title="Blue-collar">blue-collar</a> workers in regions such as northern <a href="/wiki/Meurthe-et-Moselle" title="Meurthe-et-Moselle">Meurthe-et-Moselle</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nord-Pas-de-Calais" title="Nord-Pas-de-Calais">Nord-Pas-de-Calais</a>, although most of these regions, despite his gains, remain reliably left-wing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The party is also strong in every election in very wealthy suburban or coastal (and, in some cases, urban) areas such as <a href="/wiki/Neuilly-sur-Seine" title="Neuilly-sur-Seine">Neuilly-sur-Seine</a> (72.6% for Sarkozy in the first round),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Saint-Tropez" title="Saint-Tropez">Saint-Tropez</a> (54.79%),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cannes" title="Cannes">Cannes</a> (48.19%)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Marcq-en-Bar%C5%93ul" title="Marcq-en-Barœul">Marcq-en-Barœul</a> (47.35%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> It is strong in most rural areas, like most conservative parties in the world, but this does not extend to the rural areas of the south of France, areas which are old strongholds of <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republican</a> and secular ideals. However, in old "clerical" Catholic rural areas, such as parts of <a href="/wiki/Loz%C3%A8re" title="Lozère">Lozère</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cantal" title="Cantal">Cantal</a>, it is very strong, as was the UDF during its hey day.</p>
<p>However, the UMP does poorly in one of the UDF's best regions, <a href="/wiki/Brittany_(administrative_region)" title="Brittany (administrative region)">Brittany</a>, where the decline of religious practice, a moderate electorate and urbanisation has hurt the UMP and also the UDF. Nicolas Sarkozy performed relatively poorly in departments with a large share of moderate Christian democratic (often centrist or centre-right) voters, such as <a href="/wiki/Loz%C3%A8re" title="Lozère">Lozère</a> where the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Party_(France)" title="Socialist Party (France)">Socialist</a> candidate <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A9gol%C3%A8ne_Royal" title="Ségolène Royal">Ségolène Royal</a> performed better (44.3%) than <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Mitterrand" title="François Mitterrand">François Mitterrand</a> had in his 1988 left-wing landslide (43.1%). While former President <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Chirac" title="Jacques Chirac">Jacques Chirac</a>, the right's strongman in normally left-wing <a href="/wiki/Corr%C3%A8ze" title="Corrèze">Corrèze</a> had always done very well in Corrèze and the surrounding departments, Sarkozy did very poorly and actually lost the department in the 2007 runoff. However, in the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2009_(France)" title="European Parliament election, 2009 (France)">2009 European election</a>, the UMP's results in those departments were superior to Sarkozy's first round result (nationally, they were 4% lower).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ifop_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-ifop-65">[65]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership">Leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-Cleanup" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vice_Presidents">Vice Presidents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_for_a_Popular_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Vice Presidents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Gaudin" title="Jean-Claude Gaudin">Jean-Claude Gaudin</a>, executive vice president (2002–2007)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Gaudin" title="Jean-Claude Gaudin">Jean-Claude Gaudin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_M%C3%A9haignerie" title="Pierre Méhaignerie">Pierre Méhaignerie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Raffarin" title="Jean-Pierre Raffarin">Jean-Pierre Raffarin</a> as vice presidents of the national council (2007–2012)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Luc_Chatel" title="Luc Chatel">Luc Chatel</a> (2012–2014), associate vice president from January 2013
<ul>
<li>joined by <a href="/wiki/Laurent_Wauquiez" title="Laurent Wauquiez">Laurent Wauquiez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Gaudin" title="Jean-Claude Gaudin">Jean-Claude Gaudin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Estrosi" title="Christian Estrosi">Christian Estrosi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brice_Hortefeux" title="Brice Hortefeux">Brice Hortefeux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Karoutchi" title="Roger Karoutchi">Roger Karoutchi</a>, <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9rard_Longuet" title="Gérard Longuet">Gérard Longuet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henri_de_Raincourt" title="Henri de Raincourt">Henri de Raincourt</a> (January 2013–May 2015)</li>
<li>joined by <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Falco" title="Hubert Falco">Hubert Falco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rachida_Dati" title="Rachida Dati">Rachida Dati</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herv%C3%A9_Gaymard" title="Hervé Gaymard">Hervé Gaymard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Kert" title="Christian Kert">Christian Kert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Lamour" title="Jean-François Lamour">Jean-François Lamour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Paul_Fournier" title="Jean-Paul Fournier">Jean-Paul Fournier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Audy" title="Jean-Pierre Audy">Jean-Pierre Audy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Peltier" title="Guillaume Peltier">Guillaume Peltier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Leonetti" title="Jean Leonetti">Jean Leonetti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thierry_Mariani" title="Thierry Mariani">Thierry Mariani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Ollier" title="Patrick Ollier">Patrick Ollier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Perrut" title="Bernard Perrut">Bernard Perrut</a> (January 2013–May 2015)</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
